Pressure Eases On Oil Supply
Pressure Eases On Oil Supply
By James Herron, Jay Solomon and Farnaz Fassihi
1,462 words
13 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A surprising boost in global oil inventories, aided by supply increases from Saudi Arabia, is blunting the
possibility that sanctions against Iran will drive up oil prices -- potentially undercutting Tehran on the eve of its
first nuclear talks with the West in more than a year.
The International Energy Agency, which represents the interests of major energy-consuming rich countries,
said on Thursday that more than two years of steadily tightening oil-market conditions have reversed.
"The cycle of repeatedly tightening fundamentals evident since 2009 has been broken for now," the IEA said.
As a result, many observers now see oil prices as stable or headed down, barring another spur from Mideast
tensions. That outlook offers hope for consumers who have suffered for months with high prices driven in part
by concerns about Iran.
The trends, market analysts say, are pointing toward lower gasoline prices into the summer and the lead-up
to the general election in the U.S.
Global oil inventories grew by as much as 1.2 million barrels a day in the first quarter, meaning that the oil
market could remain balanced this summer even as almost one million barrels a day of Iranian oil is taken off
the market by sanctions, the IEA said.
A subsequent report from the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries on Thursday concluded that oil
markets are well supplied.
The assessments are the result of several factors. Production from OPEC ran ahead of demand, in large part
because of increased production and storage by Saudi Arabia -- though the world's top exporter insists it has
no plans to replace Iranian oil. China, too, has stockpiled oil.
The shifting landscape comes into focus on the eve of talks between Iran and six world powers -- the U.S.,
Russia, China, Germany, the U.K. and France -- that are set to begin in Istanbul this weekend. As sanctions
loom, easing price pressures could strengthen the hand of the nations pressuring Iran to abandon what the
U.S. and others say is its aim to develop nuclear weapons, a charge Tehran denies.
U.S. and European diplomats are cautious about the prospects for success in their first negotiations with Iran
over its nuclear program since talks broke down in January 2011. But these officials said they believe the
West's leverage over Tehran has significantly increased thanks to financial and economic sanctions imposed
on Iran over the past six months.
President Barack Obama signed into law legislation that bars U.S. and foreign firms from dealings with Iran's
central bank, the main conduit for Iranian oil sales. And the European Union is putting in place an embargo
on Iranian oil purchases. Both measures go into effect at the end of June, but have already led many
countries to dial back or sever its business with Iran.
In a sign the U.S. thinks its measures are working, Secretary of State Hillary Clinton said on Thursday in
Washington that, heading into this weekend's talks, the U.S. is "receiving signals that [the Iranians] are
bringing ideas to the table."
Iran, the world's fourth-largest oil producer, has long viewed its oil reserves as a leverage point with the West
-- and has shown confidence that the West needs its oil as much as it needs the revenue.
"The global economy will pay the price for sanctioning Iran's oil," Seyed Shamsedin Hosseini, Iran's finance
minister and the government's spokesperson for economy, said on Tuesday. "The consequences of this
embargo will disrupt the oil market, not Iran. We can always find other buyers."

--- Page 77 ---
Page 77 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But such market unrest has not materialized. Oil prices leapt in the first quarter, as fears of an economic
meltdown in Europe receded and tension over Iran's nuclear program raised the perceived risk of a sudden
supply disruption. The price of Brent crude, the European benchmark that many global oil purchases are tied
to, shot up 14% in the period.
This month, however, crude has largely moved sideways, as oil buyers adjusted to Iranian sanctions and
turned back to a middling economic outlook. U.S. prices are down 5.6% from their 2012 high in February. And
prices are lower than they were a year ago, when war in Libya curtailed supplies and U.S. unemployment
was higher.
Oil prices rose across the board on Thursday despite the IEA's report, with traders taking cues from a
stronger stock market and a drop in the dollar, closing 0.92% higher in New York at $103.64 per barrel. A
weaker dollar typically boosts prices of the dollar-denominated commodity.
Barring a geopolitical spur, such as a spike in tensions with Iran, prices could be relatively stagnant for a few
months, said Amrita Sen, an energy analyst at Barclays. "It does look as if the oil market has opted for a
period of quiet reflection," the bank said this week.
That suggests savings at the pump. "It seems inevitable with slowing economic growth and increasing crude
supply" that oil and gasoline prices will head downward, says Garfield Miller, president of Aegis Energy
Advisors.
Prices are already easing at U.S. gas stations. The national average price for a gallon of regular unleaded fell
to $3.907 on Thursday, down nearly 3 cents from a week ago, according to AAA. Prices generally climb in the
spring in anticipation of more driving during warm weather, but this year the climb started earlier --
mid-December -- because of concerns over Iranian oil supplies and the closing of a number of money-losing
East Coast refineries.
Thursday's reports from the IEA and OPEC buttressed the view that relative calm is ahead.
The IEA said Iran's crude exports were beginning to tumble under the weight of sanctions. The country's
average oil production in the first quarter was down more than 350,000 barrels a day, or 9.7%, compared with
the same period in 2011. OPEC, in its monthly report, estimated a decrease of 7.1% in Iranian production in
the first quarter.
Iran's oil production is in long-term decline, but the reduction seen by the IEA last quarter was almost 20
times larger than the average year-on-year decrease in the same period in 2011 and 2010, suggesting that
sanctions are having a marked effect on the country's ability to sell oil. Based on the average selling price for
Iran's main heavy export crude, this fall in production would have deprived the country of around $3 billion in
revenue over the quarter.
Iran's National Oil Company has admitted that demand for the country's oil had dropped. Mohsen Ghamsari,
the international director for the National Oil Company, told Sharq newspaper on Saturday that Iran was
facing trouble negotiating new oil contracts and receiving payments because of tough international sanctions.
Yet the IEA and OPEC played down the impact on markets of lost Iranian production.
David Fyfe, the head of IEA's oil markets division, said the situation wouldn't create a need for a major draw
on oil inventories in rich countries or offsetting output from other producers. He cautioned, however, that the
release of emergency oil stocks -- which the U.S., the U.K. and France are contemplating -- couldn't be ruled
out.
One reason the market will remain in balance this summer, Mr. Fyfe said, is that OPEC members have
substantially increased production, "with one eye on what might happen with the potential loss of Iranian
supplies."
Total OPEC oil production rose by 135,000 barrels a day to 31.43 million barrels a day in March, largely
because of higher Iraqi output, the IEA report said.
Daily output from the group has risen by almost 1.4 million barrels over the last six months. Around a third of
this increase comes from Saudi Arabia's spare production capacity, with improvements in Iraq and Libya's oil
industry delivering the rest.
Despite its professed neutrality in the standoff between Iran and the West, Saudi Arabia -- at the urging of the
U.S. -- has been flexing its oil muscles. Soon after European officials started discussing a ban in December,
the Saudis revealed they had abruptly increased their oil production to a 30-year high.

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Saudis went a step further in March, saying they had filled up their domestic oil inventories and placed a
further 10 million barrels of oil in storage close to markets in Europe and Japan. This means the Saudis are
well placed to meet extra demand for their oil from refiners as the July sanctions approach, the IEA said.

Wedding Dues Lead to Don'ts
Wedding Dues Lead to Don'ts
By Michael Howard Saul
886 words
13 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Bride-to-be Jenn Jarvis thought she had found the perfect venue for her wedding this year: Brooklyn Bridge
Park, a waterfront expanse with emerald lawns and sweeping views of Manhattan, all for just $25.
"Our awesome caterers, event planners, said, 'It will be gorgeous and lovely, and it's only a $25 permit fee,' "
she said.
She soon learned, however, that on Dec. 1, the nonrefundable fee quietly jumped to $425. While the increase
wasn't the last straw that prodded Ms. Jarvis and her fiance to hold the wedding elsewhere, it was a "pretty
big straw," she said.
"It's not like they added $10 for inflation," Ms. Jarvis said. "Maybe in the long run $400 isn't that much when
you consider the location, but to go from $25 to $425 seems pretty unreasonable to me."
As wedding season gets under way, many couples are learning that exchanging vows in a public park can
come with a host of bureaucratic do's and don'ts.
In most New York City parks, there is a $25 permit fee for gatherings of 20 people or more. Two locations --
Brooklyn Bridge Park and the Conservatory Garden in Central Park -- charge an additional $400. And for
couples who pay online for a Brooklyn Bridge Park permit, there is an extra $1 processing fee.
And that is just to say "I do." Couples who got married elsewhere but just want the park's dramatic backdrop
for their wedding pictures may also need to pay for a special permit. At Brooklyn Bridge Park, the photo fee is
$26, and at the Conservatory Garden, it is $100.
Still, they aren't the city's most expensive options. At Gantry Plaza State Park, a 12-acre riverside plot in Long
Island City, Queens, wedding permits range from $800 to $1,600; if the bride and groom want chairs,
decorations and sound equipment, it could cost more.
An after-hours event on Liberty or Ellis islands, both run by the National Park Service, costs up to $20,000 for
1,200 guests, with security, transportation and catering extra. The no-frills option -- an hourlong standing
ceremony with 75 people -- is $75.
Ellen Ryan, a spokeswoman for Brooklyn Bridge Park, called the fee hike there "reasonable."
The park, which is run by a nonprofit, doesn't receive government money for maintenance and operations,
she said.
"In exchange for using a terrific public place for a wedding ceremony, $400 is a terrific contribution to keeping
the park beautiful," she said. "It helps with staffing. It helps with upkeep of the lawns."
Plenty of couples are willing to pay. Already this year there have been 110 weddings or wedding-related
permits issued at Brooklyn Bridge Park.
The lush Conservatory Garden at 105th Street and Fifth Avenue hosts about 10 to 15 weddings a week from
April to November, said Dena Libner, a spokeswoman for the Central Park Conservancy. The wedding fee
increased to $400 from $100 about nine years ago, she said. The fee is a "reflection of the sheer demand" for
the garden as a backdrop, she said, and the "enormous work" required to maintain it.
When couples show up without a permit, Ms. Libner acknowledged, "it's very touchy."
"We would politely tell them that a permit is required for photography in that section, and if they were unable
to pay it . . . we would gladly point out some other locations at the park that are just as beautiful," she said.

--- Page 80 ---
Page 80 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Enforcing the permits can be an art. A woman in a flowing white dress might catch the attention of park
officials, while more simply attired brides might not. Brooklyn Bridge Park requires a permit only for
"extensive" shoots involving, for instance, a lengthy setup time or equipment, Ms. Ryan said.
On Saturday, Jason Lapuebla, a 28-year-old photographer from the Bronx, was shooting a couple at the
Conservatory Garden without a permit. He said he's never been chased away, but in the past he has been
asked whether he has a permit.
"Sometimes, even if I'm just shooting something personal with a friend, police or park people in the city will
ask for a permit," he said.
After permits are obtained, couples often still have to abide by a host of restrictions.
Michelle Wiener of Brooklyn considered having her wedding at Brooklyn Bridge Park, but she ultimately found
the tight rules -- not the fee -- too onerous.
No staging, microphones, tables, podiums, tents or loud music can be used for the ceremony. Only five
folding chairs can be brought. No more than 100 people are allowed.
Ms. Wiener said it was too much. "If I were to pay $400 to use a corner of the park for an hour or less, I
should be able to provide the means for guests to be comfortable and hear the ceremony," she said.
Ms. Jarvis, who lives in Brooklyn, said she's now planning an October wedding in Curacao in the Caribbean.
She doesn't yet have a final price tag. The venue may cost more than $400, she conceded, but in total it will
cost "less than it would have cost to do the wedding in Brooklyn."

Dow Has Its Best Day in a Month
By Jonathan Cheng and Matt Jarzemsky

The bulls are back. At least for now.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average surged 181.19 points, or 1.41%, to 12986.58 on Thursday, its biggest gain
in a month, propelled by investors' positive interpretation of speeches by senior Federal Reserve officials and
hopes of stronger-than-expected growth in China.
Those hopes about China were not realized: The country's annual growth came in at 8.1%, its lowest pace
since the first quarter of 2009. The numbers were slightly higher than Chinese government estimates but
lower than many economists were expecting.
In the past two days, the Dow is up 2.1%, its strongest back-to-back performance this year.
The gains have helped the Dow pick up some of the ground lost over the previous five sessions as investors
dumped stocks amid signs of renewed troubles in Europe and concerns that the U.S. economic recovery may
be slowing.
Stock-market bulls say they aren't overlooking those concerns but that the selling was likely a temporary
pullback rather than the beginning of a downward slide.
Companies are in a strong financial position and stock valuations are still reasonable, said John Carey,
portfolio manager at Pioneer Investments.
"The underlying trend is still positive," said Mr. Carey, who helps manage more than $200 billion in global
assets at Pioneer. "I'm still optimistic."
Still, there were signs the recent surge wasn't fueled by the U.S. economy.
Many investors were reacting to comments by Fed Vice Chairman Janet Yellen and New York Fed President
William Dudley, who both highlighted the risks to the U.S. economy. That suggested to some investors that
the Fed may be more amenable to extending its low-interest-rate policies, which has helped propel stocks
higher.
"There may be more hope in the market for additional quantitative easing down the road," said Ryan Larson,
head of equity trading at RBC Global Asset Management.
A report Thursday showed weekly jobless claims rose to the highest level since late January. The previous
week's number also was revised higher, by 10,000, suggesting that the recent economic improvement may
be slowing.
Thursday's U.S. stock-market advance was propelled by materials, energy and industrials stocks.
"People are thinking that the pullback we experienced is maybe enough for now," said Reed Choate, portfolio
manager at asset manager Neville, Rodie Shaw in New York.
"Nothing's been solved in the long-term story, but there's so much liquidity in the market that has missed this
rally or has been sitting on the sidelines that people are coming back in to invest."
In a sign of broad optimism, the number of New York Stock Exchange-listed companies posting gains
outnumbered losers by a ratio of 4.6 to 1, making for the broadest-based stock advance in more than two
months.

--- Page 82 ---
Page 82 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
U.S. company earnings also are playing a role. In the first three months of the year, Wall Street analysts
tamped down their profits expectations so much that investors now are preparing for positive surprises from
corporate America.
Alcoa began earnings season with an upbeat report. Hewlett-Packard on Thursday gained 7.2% after an
upbeat report on personal computer sales.
Meanwhile, Google on Thursday surpassed estimates. Google rose $15.05 a share, or 2.4%, to $651.01 per
share in trading Thursday.
"If these companies start reporting better-than-expected earnings, you're going to see this market push back
up to old highs," Mr. Choate said.
Adding to the optimism, a report Thursday on U.S. exports showed the trade deficit contracting in February at
its sharpest rate in nearly three years.
While trade numbers rarely have a big impact on stocks, the strength in exports has bolstered hopes for U.S.

"American companies are exporting more, and that feeds into the earnings story," Mr. Gendreau said. "If you
were worried that a slowdown in global growth was going to hurt earnings, this number suggests that it's not
going to be quite as bad as it would be."
Meantime, in a sign of easing fears in Europe, government borrowing rates for Italy and Spain tumbled for a

starting to believe the Fed may add another round of easing. The dollar tends to fall when expectations for
loose monetary policy rise.
That expectation helped drive up the prices of commodities, which are denominated in dollars. Oil rose 0.9%
to bring its two-day gain to 2.6%, while gold rose 1.2% to a two-week high.

The Boys on Le Bus: Foreign Press Mines Local Angles in U.S. Primary --- Interviewing Santorum's Italian Cugini; Ron Paul Yard Signs in...
The Boys on Le Bus: Foreign Press Mines Local Angles in U.S. Primary --- Interviewing Santorum's
Italian Cugini; Ron Paul Yard Signs in Stockholm
By Elizabeth Williamson
1,176 words
10 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
While the U.S. press has been scrutinizing Mitt Romney's business record, British reporters have focused on
an entirely different angle of the Republican presidential primary: Ann Romney's roots in a Welsh coal mining
village.
In a dispatch the day before the New Hampshire primary, the Daily Mail detailed the tribulations of Mrs.
Romney's grandfather, a coal miner in the Welsh village of Coegnant Colliery: "When a coal cart crushed into
him he lost a kidney," the story explained, "leaving the family worried for their future."
The Welsh coal mining connection appears to be an asset for Mr. Romney, the story continued. "Whether it is
enough to secure the Republican nomination for Mr. Romney, only time will tell."
Covering the endless U.S. presidential primary is a particular challenge for the 1,200 foreign journalists
swarming the trail this year. They rarely win attention, let alone interviews, from the candidates. Moreover,
their readers overseas often view the contest as if through the opposite end of a telescope: a race toward a
barely perceptible goal featuring inscrutable issues and a shifting cast of characters.
That is why, even though they work alongside their U.S. counterparts, foreign reporters seem to inhabit a
parallel universe, mining the contest and the candidates' biographies for anything remotely resonant to
various domestic audiences.
During the Florida primaries, the U.S. press featured a faceoff over immigration and public finances between
Mr. Romney and a newly resurgent Newt Gingrich. The Irish Times, however led its website with a different
focus: "Gingrich Vows Permanent Moonbase," the Times proclaimed, referring to the former House speaker's
plan to colonize the moon by 2020.
Rick Santorum, who talks of the hardworking Italian grandfather who inspired him, prompted the Italian
magazine Oggi to find distant cousins in his ancestral town of Riva del Garda. One of the cugini told the
publication that Mr. Santorum's grandfather and uncles were members of Italy's postwar communist party.
They were "communists to the core," he said.
Mr. Santorum's spokesman Hogan Gidley declined to comment on the Italian's observation. "You're not
writing that he's some kind of socialist, are you?" Mr. Gidley asked.
Veronika Oleksyn, an American-born freelance correspondent for Austria's Kurier daily, thought she had
written as much as she could about Ron Paul's connection to Austria: He adheres to the Austrian School of
economic thought, which espouses, among other things, reinstituting the gold standard and abolishing the
central bank.
Then she learned that the Texas congressman's favorite movie is "The Sound of Music," the stylized saga of
the Austrian von Trapp Family Singers, who eventually settled in the Super Tuesday primary state of
Vermont. "I got really excited," she said.
After some research, however, she discovered that "no one in Austria really knows what 'The Sound of Music'
is." She didn't write a story.
U.S. reporters grouse about how their overseas counterparts waste time with esoteric questions. During one
of Mr. Romney's rare press availabilities, foreign reporters asked about whether he would return the bust of
Winston Churchill removed by President Barack Obama to the Oval Office and what he thinks of the
U.S.-Brazil relationship.

--- Page 85 ---
Page 85 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"He looked at me and answered 'It's sad that Obama has forgotten about Latin America,'" said reporter
Denise Crispim of O Estado de S. Paulo, who asked the Brazil question. "I thought, 'Uh huh, he doesn't know
anything about Brazil.'"
A spokeswoman for Mr. Romney dismissed the comment and said the former governor is deeply aware of
Brazil's economic and security issues.
The Ron Paul press operation, the most open and easygoing in the Republican race, has disappointed the
Dutch and the Scandinavians. They are enamored with the Texas congressman's noninterventionist foreign
policy but haven't been granted any interviews with the candidate, via email or in person.
"They show us pictures of Ron Paul yard signs in their country and say 'See, there are signs and stickers all

dealing with foreign press."
Olivier O'Mahony, bureau chief of the popular French magazine Paris Match, said he hasn't been able to get
his editors interested in big, splashy features on the U.S. presidential primary.
The closest Mr. O'Mahony has gotten: a feature about former Arkansas legislator Jim Bob Duggar, his wife
and their family of 19 home-schooled children. The magazine devoted six color pages to the Duggar family,
who were on the road in Iowa campaigning for Mr. Santorum.
"This is such an American story -- in the French mind," said Mr. O'Mahony of the Duggars. He reported the

show called "19 Kids and Counting" on TLC.

gentleman, and our family has a motto: There's always room for one more."
Sensing a potential diplomatic setback in all the campaign snubs to foreign press, the U.S. State Department
has stepped in to help. The department's Foreign Press Center offers overseas journalists tightly
orchestrated, foreigners-only access to the American primary process.
U.S. diplomats with experience in foreign hot spots lead scores of foreign journalists on tours of the nation's
electoral landscape. On the itinerary: introductions to local politicos, voters suffering from the foreclosure
crisis, and special interest groups like "actual Florida seniors," according to a State Department description.
Naoufal Enhari, Washington correspondent for Agence Maghreb Arabe Presse, Morocco's news service,
says the tours expose him to people he would never find on his own, including some who still erroneously
believe Mr. Obama is Muslim. "I was a little bit shocked," he said. In his majority-Muslim country, "people
think everyone in the U.S. is a Christian."
Kumi Okoe, a Japanese television commentator and fellow at Washington's Heritage Foundation, said every
one of Japan's electronic and print media outlets has a team on the campaign trail. "There is so much
competition that if one company goes to an event, the other companies will go," Ms. Okoe said. "Even if they
don't use the story, they all accumulate all the news."
Not using the story happens pretty often this year, she said. "In 2008 we had a campaign between an
African-American and Hillary Clinton, a white woman who was already famous."
This time, with President Obama competing against a field unknown in Tokyo, "We cover everything, but
Japanese TV doesn't show the tape."

Notable & Quotable
Notable & Quotable
346 words
7 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
From "Economics in One Lesson," by Henry Hazlitt (1946):
Even a relatively mild inflation distorts the structure of production. It leads to the over-expansion of some
industries at the expense of others. This involves a misapplication and waste of capital. When the inflation
collapses, or is brought to a halt, the misdirected capital investment -- whether in the form of machines,
factories or office buildings -- cannot yield an adequate return and loses the greater part of its value.
Nor is it possible to bring inflation to a smooth and gentle stop, and so avert a subsequent depression. It is
not even possible to halt an inflation, once embarked upon, at some preconceived point, or when prices
have achieved a previously-agreed-upon level; for both political and economic forces, will have got out of
hand. . . .
For . . . the causation is never a merely mechanical one. You cannot, for example, say in advance that a 100
per cent increase in the quantity of money will mean a 50 per cent fall in the value of the monetary unit. The
value of money, as we have seen, depends upon the subjective valuations of the people who hold it. And
those valuations do not depend solely on the quantity of it that each person holds. They depend also on the
quality of the money. In wartime the value of a nation's monetary unit, not on the gold standard, will rise on
the foreign exchanges with victory and fall with defeat, regardless of changes in its quantity. The present
valuation will often depend upon what people expect the future quantity of money to be. And, as with
commodities on the speculative exchanges, each person's valuation of money is affected not only by what he
thinks its value is but by what he thinks is going to be everybody else's valuation of money.
All this explains why, when super-inflation has once set in, the value of the monetary unit drops at a far
faster rate than the quantity of money either is or can be increased.

Markets Fear End of Stimulus --- Global Stocks Sink on Worries Central Bankers in Europe, U.S. Won't Act Further
Markets Fear End of Stimulus --- Global Stocks Sink on Worries Central Bankers in Europe, U.S. Won't
Act Further
By Jonathan Cheng and Charles Forelle
1,080 words
5 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Fears that the central banks of Europe and the U.S. may soon end efforts to support financial markets as well
as fresh concerns about the health of Europe's weakest countries drove down stock markets around the
world Wednesday.
European Central Bank President Mario Draghi indicated he would be hesitant to undertake more monetary
easing, citing concerns about inflation. That surprised investors who had been relying on the ECB to help
support the region's economy and financial markets.
Adding to concerns, an auction Wednesday of Spanish government bonds was met with surprisingly
lackluster demand. The disappointing sale was a reminder to investors that Europe's problems are far from
over, and prices of European sovereign bonds fell, sending yields higher. The euro also dropped against the
dollar.
Stock indexes from New York to Frankfurt to Tokyo fell sharply. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 124.80
points to 13074.75, its worst day since March 6. In Germany, the DAX index dropped 2.8%, its biggest
one-day fall in a month. Japan's Nikkei Stock Average tumbled 2.3%, its worst day since Nov. 10. Stocks
were down in Japan and other Asian markets early Thursday.
The slide began in the U.S. Tuesday after signs from the Federal Reserve that it won't immediately embark
on a new round of bond buying. That dashed hopes of investors who had anticipated a new program would
further juice financial markets and the economy.
"People thought that central banks could solve all of our problems and that we could forget all about Europe,"
said Jerry Webman, chief economist of New York-based OppenheimerFunds, which manages $184 billion in
assets. "The weak Spanish auction tells us that these risks in Europe are still there. The markets are right to
be a bit cautious about how the European debt situation evolves."
Investors are divided about whether the declines of the past two days are merely an overdue pullback, or
whether it is the beginning of a longer-term slump. Many financial markets had their best first quarter in years,
a reason some investors say sets them up for further declines.
The ECB's program to supply liquidity to the region's banks helped fuel a rally in sovereign debt as well as
European stocks and the euro. In the U.S., the Dow had its best start to a year since 1998, thanks to an
improving U.S. economy and expectations that the Fed will again pump money into the financial system.
The Dow's triple-digit decline was just the second this year.
"You have a sign that the Fed is very unlikely to keep this liquidity coming, and people are starting to believe
that Draghi is serious, and you start to ask yourself the question: What happens if that goes away?" said Dan
Alpert, managing partner at Westwood Capital LLP.
Patrick Gundlach, vice president and portfolio manager at BMO Global Asset Management in Milwaukee,
said this week's stock market weakness isn't surprising and could continue, though central banks will be
reluctant to let markets slide.
"The central banks are still saying they won't let things fall apart," Mr. Gundlach said.
The ECB's program in large part enabled banks in the region to gobble up debt of their sovereign country.
That helped keep demand high at recent auctions, but also may have masked waning interest among other
buyers.
Wednesday brought those fears to the forefront.

--- Page 88 ---
Page 88 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Spain sold 2.59 billion euros ($3.43 billion) in bonds with maturities between 2015 and 2020, near the bottom
of its target volume, and it had to pay higher yields than in previous auctions to entice investors to take them.
The auction was a first verdict on the new government's just-announced budget plan, which calls for severe
spending cuts and tax increases to trim a persistent deficit.
Demand from Spanish banks is "much lower than in January and February," said Laurent Fransolet of
Barclays PLC in London. It wasn't likely, he said, that local banks could have "continued to buy 20 billion
[euros] of bonds a month." Foreign investors may also be skeptical of Spain's fiscal plans. "There are a lot of
question marks about the banking system and the budget," Mr. Fransolet said.
As of February, Spanish banks held 142 billion euros, or 25%, of so-called unstripped government debt, or
securities whose interest and principal payments haven't been split into separate instruments, according to
data from the Spanish treasury.
That was up from 94 billion euros in December. In the mid-2000s, the Spanish banks' holdings were typically
around 6% of the total.
Italy, too, saw substantial domestic buying in early 2012, and there are signs there, as well, of a falloff in
demand. Secondary-market yields on Italian debt have risen markedly since bottoming out in early March.
Weak demand could be a particular problem for Italy, which plans to issue 56 billion euros in bonds in the
second quarter.
Some investors said the lackluster demand could also be chalked up to the impending extended Easter
break, which begins this week.
At the end of European trading, yields on the Spanish 10-year bond were at 5.71%; they had ended Tuesday
at 5.45%, the highest since early January, according to Tradeweb. The comparable Italian bond was at
5.38%, up from 5.15%.
"I think the global financial community is out of patience with Spain, and frankly, with Europe in general for
setting expectations at a certain level and under-delivering," said Jim Russell, chief equity strategist with US
Bank Wealth Management. "The last thing investors need is another go-round with Europe."
The declines came despite signs the U.S. economy is improving. Private-sector jobs data showed 209,000
jobs were added in March. That is a precursor to the Labor Department's nonfarm payrolls report on Friday.
Commodities, another beneficiary of central-bank easing and an improving economy, also tumbled
Wednesday. Gold lost 3.5%, its biggest decline in one month. Silver dropped 6.7%, while copper, an
industrial metal tied to global growth, fell 3.3%. Crude oil lost 2.4%, bringing prices to $101.47 a barrel, the
lowest level since Feb. 14.

Precious metals tumbled, with gold ending at a 12-week low, as investors reacted to the Federal Reserve's

in concert.

could rise before 2014 in meeting minutes released after floor trading ended Tuesday. Previous stimulus
measures had sent gold prices soaring as worries about weak paper currencies and potential inflation flared
up.

continuing through Wednesday.

Trading, referring to a potential third round of Federal Reserve asset purchases, or quantitative easing.

Investing in Funds: A Quarterly Analysis --- Mixing It Up: How to Select a Dividend-Focused Fund --- Look for those that spread their money...
Investing in Funds: A Quarterly Analysis --- Mixing It Up: How to Select a Dividend-Focused Fund ---
Look for those that spread their money across numerous sectors, this advisory firm suggests
By Shefali Anand
946 words
5 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Individuals should be wary when picking funds that invest in stocks that pay big dividends, says Dave Foster,
a financial adviser in Cincinnati.
A fund that targets high dividend yields could be invested in stocks from only a handful of industries -- say,
utilities and consumer staples, says Mr. Foster. A turn in the fate of one of the industries could lead to sharp
swings in the value of the fund.
In 2008, for example, many dividend-oriented mutual funds had large investments in financial companies,
which were big dividend payers at the time. After the financial meltdown, these companies cut their dividends
across the board and their shares lost more value than other dividend-paying stocks.
"What I would look for is funds . . . that are well diversified with sectors," says Mr. Foster.
In this column, we feature model portfolios from prominent financial advisers using mutual funds and
exchange-traded funds. Mr. Foster has been giving financial advice for more than 25 years. In 1996 he
co-founded Foster & Motley Inc., which recently managed around $720 million in assets, primarily in
individual securities.
Mr. Foster has long used dividend-paying stocks, partly to create an income stream for retired clients. But a
high dividend isn't his only criteria for a stock or fund. "Our portfolio doesn't have a 4.5% yield," which would
be quite rich today, he says. Instead, the firm expects a modestly higher yield than that of the Standard &
Poor's 500-stock index, now about 2% -- without taking lots more risk.
His firm looks for funds that own high-quality shares across industries that aim to increase dividends over
time, and that are trading at a reasonable price. The investment team says one such fund is Vanguard
Dividend Growth, which recently had 25% of its portfolio in consumer staples and industrials. They also like
Nuveen Dividend Value and Shelton Core Value.
Here is a model mutual-fund portfolio used by the firm for moderate-risk clients. The featured portfolio has a
weighted average expense ratio of 0.6%, and the adviser charges a fee of 0.75% or less depending on the
client's assets.
U.S. STOCKS: A 38% allocation goes to U.S. stocks, of which more than three-quarters are large-cap and
the rest small.
The firm's advisers tend not to change target allocations in response to market conditions. Thom Guidi, a
portfolio manager at the firm, thinks tactical bets don't aid long-term returns.
Vanguard Dividend Growth, which currently yields 2.1%, gets a 20% allocation. Ten percent goes to Schwab
Fundamental U.S. Large Company Index, which tracks an index that weights factors like sales and cash flows
instead of just stock-market values. Mr. Guidi likes the fund's sector-neutral approach.
For small-company stocks, Mr. Guidi allocates 8% to DFA U.S. Small Cap Value. The fund buys stocks it
considers inexpensive and holds them a long time.
FOREIGN STOCKS: The portfolio has a 12% allocation to foreign stocks. Mr. Guidi allocates 4% to Harbor
International and 3% to Scout International, both of which invest primarily in stocks of developed countries but
also have developing-country stocks.
There is a 5% allocation to DFA Emerging Markets Value, which buys shares in developing markets like
Brazil and China.

--- Page 91 ---
Page 91 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Guidi expects to allocate more to emerging markets over the next few years. "That's just a recognition
that emerging markets make up a larger proportion of the world economy," he says.
BONDS: The portfolio has 35% in bonds. Mr. Guidi invests 18% in high-quality corporate bonds, because the
gap between yields of these bonds and U.S. Treasurys is larger than historical norms. He uses the iShares
Barclays Intermediate Credit Bond ETF, which currently owns corporate bonds with an average duration of
around four years.
Ten percent of the portfolio is in Vanguard GNMA, which buys mortgage securities backed by the U.S.
government and currently has a yield of 2.7%.
There's a 7% allocation to Loomis Sayles Bond, which can own high- and low-quality bonds and foreign
bonds. Mr. Guidi uses it partly to get exposure to lower-quality, high-yield bonds.
ALTERNATIVES: The advisers allocate 15% to alternative investments. In this model, Mr. Guidi has six such
funds, which he hopes together will "provide a near-zero correlation to the stock market" and an overall
higher return than the bond portion. "The alternatives are there to reduce volatility," he says.
There is a 5% allocation to Vanguard REIT Index ETF, which buys stocks issued by real-estate investment
trusts, companies that buy office buildings, hotels and other property.
Five other funds each get a 2% allocation. Absolute Strategies uses varied tactics, including buying bonds of
companies on the brink of bankruptcy and seeking to profit when stocks fall. Pimco All Asset All Authority
aims to preserve capital and maximize after-inflation returns. Federated Prudent Bear gains value when
stocks lose. This fund hasn't done well lately because stocks have risen.
Mr. Guidi uses a managed-futures fund, MutualHedge Frontier Legends, which owns commodities, currencies
and other derivatives.
For inflation protection, he buys PowerShares DB Commodity Index Tracking, because commodities
typically benefit in times of rising prices. For clients with larger assets, he buys a dedicated gold fund as well.
---
Ms. Anand is a personal-finance columnist for The Wall Street Journal, based in New Delhi. Email:
shefali.anand@wsj.com.

Investing in Funds: A Quarterly Analysis --- Tax Pitfalls for Fund Investors --- Avoiding these five mistakes can save you grief and put...
Investing in Funds: A Quarterly Analysis --- Tax Pitfalls for Fund Investors --- Avoiding these five
mistakes can save you grief and put more money in your pocket
By Rachel Louise Ensign
1,790 words
5 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Fund investors can go wrong in all sorts of ways. But since mid-April is fast approaching, let's talk about one
of the most common and least understood: taxes.
Even if it is too late to do anything about this year's returns, it is a good time to start planning for next year's.
At the root of the most common blunders are three types of taxable fund payouts: interest income, dividends
and capital gains. While all three are subject to a complex web of tax rates and regulations, investors can limit
their tax bills by understanding their funds, planning carefully and staying abreast of tax changes in
Washington.
Here, according to financial advisers, are five of the biggest mistakes many fund investors make:
1. KEEPING 'TAX-INEFFICIENT' FUNDS IN A TAXABLE BROKERAGE ACCOUNT
Some types of funds distribute lots of dividends, interest income and capital gains, all of which can boost tax
bills. Many investors would be better off holding those funds in tax-sheltered retirement accounts. With a
standard 401(k) plan or individual retirement account, you pay tax only when you make withdrawals; earnings
and withdrawals usually are tax-free in a Roth 401(k) or Roth IRA.
Tax-efficient funds -- those unlikely to make big distributions -- can be left in a taxable account, says Michael
Gibney, a financial adviser in Riverdale, N.J. You will owe capital-gains tax if you sell those securities at a
gain, but at least the timing of such sales is under your control.
Taxable-bond funds, including high-yield funds and funds holding Treasury inflation-protected securities, are
among the investments you might consider holding in an IRA, advisers say. Ditto for funds that emphasize
high-dividend stocks. Meanwhile, index funds that track a broad stock-market benchmark -- and most but not
all ETFs -- might be candidates for a taxable account, as would municipal bond funds, since interest earned is
tax-free.
Determining whether a fund is going to have capital gains can be tricky. Each year, funds must distribute
gains if portfolio managers sell securities for a net taxable gain. One indicator is the level of turnover in the
portfolio, though, admittedly, it is an imprecise gauge.
The higher a fund's turnover, a figure that can be found on Morningstar.com, the more likely it is to pay out
capital gains, says Mark Armbruster, president of Armbruster Capital Management, which is in the Rochester,
N.Y., area. If a fund has paid out capital gains in the past, something that also can be found on Morningstar,
that also is a sign it may do so again, he says.
Small-stock funds may produce more capital gains than large-stock funds, advisers say, because there are
many more small stocks to trade among.
Broad index funds, which don't change holdings often, are less likely to pay out gains than some actively
managed funds that change their investments based on market conditions. The Vanguard 500 Index fund, for
example, has a 4% turnover ratio and hasn't distributed capital gains since 1999. The actively managed CGM
Focus, on the other hand, has a nearly 500% turnover rate. It has performed poorly in recent years, so it
hasn't been in a position to distribute gains, but it distributed $8.21 a share in mostly short-term capital gains
in 2007.
Still, when and why a fund realizes capital gains is complex, so "turnover is only a very rough gauge of tax
efficiency," says Christine Benz, director of personal finance at Morningstar. Another gauge is Morningstar's
"potential capital-gains exposure" statistic, an estimate of the percentage of a fund's assets that represent
mostly unrealized gains.

--- Page 93 ---
Page 93 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
ETFs, in particular, rarely distribute capital gains, Mr. Armbruster says. That is because most are index funds
but also because they are structured to minimize taxable sales of portfolio securities.
2. HOLDING ON TO FUNDS THAT COST YOU BIG
Capital gains, whether taken on purpose by the investor or passed along by a fund, can add to your tax bill.
But you can lessen their impact by strategically booking capital losses when holdings decline in value, so that
they offset any gains dollar for dollar. In any year, if your capital losses exceed your capital gains, you can
take up to $3,000 of the loss as a tax deduction and carry the rest of the loss forward to offset gains in future
years.
This "tax-loss harvesting" has to be done carefully, however, to comply with Internal Revenue Service rules.
Once you sell a fund or other security at a loss, you have to wait 30 days before buying either that same fund
or a very similar fund (for instance, one that tracks the same index), or the loss is invalidated. "The securities
cannot be 'substantially identical,'" says Gil Charney, principal tax researcher at the Tax Institute at H&R
Block, a division of H&R Block Inc., but "the IRS never clearly defined what substantially identical means. . . .
It's gray."
If you want to keep exposure to the sector that fund covered, you can buy a slightly different fund -- for
instance, you likely could sell a fund tracking the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index and immediately buy one
tracking the Russell 1000, says Mr. Armbruster. You could later return to your original holding.
Keep tax-loss harvesting in mind any time the market or a particular holding suffers a major decline; you'll
miss opportunities if you think about this only near year-end.
3. BUYING AN ETF WITHOUT LEARNING WHAT ITS TAX TREATMENT IS
Gains and income from certain ETFs are subject to funky tax rules because of the funds' holdings or their
corporate structures. Though most of these aberrations invest in niche industries, some of the most popular
ETFs could leave you with a surprisingly large tax bill.
The most popular offender: Gains from selling SPDR Gold Shares, the second-largest exchange-traded
product by assets, are taxed at a top 28% rate on collectibles, rather than the maximum 15% rate on
long-term capital gains. That is true for all other funds that hold physical precious metals.
There are different rules for ETFs that provide commodities exposure by investing in futures contracts: Gains
are taxed 60% at a long-term rate and 40% at a short-term rate. ETFs structured this way include some from
the U.S. Commodity, PowerShares and ProShares families.
Also, some non-stock ETFs are structured as partnerships and report their tax information on a Schedule K-1
instead of the common 1099 form. Schedule K-1 typically is sent later than a 1099 -- it may not even arrive
before your tax return is due because the partnership has to file its own return before sending you this form,
says Eric Smith, an IRS spokesman. In this situation, you'll want to ask for an extension from the IRS, he
says. You can avoid these hassles by holding these funds in an IRA.
4. FUDGING THE NEW FORMS
Reporting securities sales on your tax return has gotten more complex, with new rules that require brokerage
firms and fund companies to report to the IRS what you paid for some securities you sell. Because that
reporting applies only to securities purchased after specified dates, you may have sales of both "covered"
and non-covered assets. As in the past, for non-covered securities, the financial firm may voluntarily provide
cost information only to you.
The new rules could make tax preparation more complex, tripping up some investors.
"Basically what they've done is taken Schedule D and added a new schedule behind it -- Form 8949. All the
transactions you used to put directly on Schedule D . . . are now on this new form," says Robert Schmansky,
a financial adviser in Bloomfield Hills, Mich.
The most important thing to know about Form 8949 is that you will have to separate the covered transactions
from those that aren't and report them on different lines. Individual stocks purchased on or after Jan. 1, 2011,
are covered; for mutual funds and most ETFs, the new treatment applies to purchases on or after Jan. 1,
2012. Then, you must add the covered and non-covered transactions and put the total on Schedule D.
5. INVESTING WITHOUT PAYING ATTENTION TO THE TAX DEBATE IN WASHINGTON
When deciding when to take gains and what account to hold various funds in, it is important to stay abreast of
what is going on in Washington.

--- Page 94 ---
Page 94 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Think hard about where tax rates are likely headed in the future. While some tax changes affecting funds are
already in store, some experts watching the political debate -- and the ballooning federal deficit -- say
investors may want to hedge their bets against higher rates and pay taxes on their gains soon.
There are a number of big tax changes on tap starting in 2013 that could deal a huge blow to your funds. If
the Bush tax cuts are allowed to expire, the top rate on ordinary income and short-term capital gains will rise
to 39.6% from 35%.
The current top 15% rate on long-term capital gains is set to rise to 20%. Qualified dividends will no longer be
taxed at a top 15% rate and will be taxed as ordinary income. Also, net investment income, which includes
dividends, interest and capital gains, will be subject to a new 3.8% Medicare tax, part of the Affordable Care
Act, for married couples filing jointly who earn more than $250,000 a year and individuals earning more than
$200,000 a year.
One possibility is that some of the current rates will be extended for most taxpayers, but not for high earners.
"People who are over the $250,000 mark -- Obama has drawn a line in the sand for those people," says Ken
Weingarten, a financial adviser in Lawrenceville, N.J. "It's going to be crazy after the election. There is going
to be a lot of horse trading to get these things straightened out."
If you think that your tax rate on capital gains will rise soon, you may want to book a capital gain this year to
lock in the 15% rate. Unlike with a capital loss, if you're booking a gain you can repurchase the same exact
fund in any quantity immediately after selling it.
---
Ms. Ensign is a staff reporter for The Wall Street Journal in New York. She can be reached at
rachel.ensign@wsj.com.

Should Rules or Discretion Drive Federal Reserve Policy?
Letters to the Editor
Should Rules or Discretion Drive Federal Reserve Policy?
525 words
5 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
In "The Dangers of an Interventionist Fed" (op-ed, March 29), Prof. John B. Taylor cites the Fed's "mistake of
slowing money growth at the onset of the Great Depression" as an example of the pitfalls of discretionary as
opposed to rule-based monetary policy.
However, much research in the intervening decades, including by Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke,
has suggested that it was a rule -- the gold standard -- which prevented the Fed from providing more liquidity
to the banking system and economy during the Great Depression. Had the Fed provided a looser monetary
policy, the link to gold could have been endangered. But severing the link to gold would have been good
policy. Once the U.S. did leave the gold standard in 1933, a recovery began.
Moreover, other countries that left the gold standard before the U.S. did experienced faster recoveries from
the Great Depression. Thus it seems in this case it wasn't discretion, but an overly rigid adherence to a rule --
the gold standard -- that led to disastrous monetary policy.
Prof. Taylor goes on to blame the Fed for encouraging the housing bubble. However, there is much evidence
that it was long-term interest rates, held low by global demand for U.S. Treasurys, over which the Fed found
it had little control (recall Alan Greenspan's "conundrum" over the inability of tighter Fed policy in 2005-06 to
raise the long-term interest rates on which mortgage borrowing costs are based) which bore more
responsibility for the housing bubble.
Sadly there is no magic bullet -- no rule, be it the gold standard, an inflation target or any other target -- that
can prevent economic and financial upheavals. Nor can any such rule replace a central bank committed to
long-run price stability, while still retaining discretion to deal with such upheavals.
William Miles
Wichita State University
Wichita, Kan.
---
Does Prof. Taylor in recommending the Sound Dollar Act actually believe that the reforms "would provide an
appropriate degree of oversight by the political authorities," and thereby enhance the economic well-being of
the U.S.? How have the "political authorities" managed fiscal policy in recent years? Giving this same group
(Congress) oversight and influence over monetary policy now would be asking for another disaster similar to
our federal debt and deficit. I'll take Mr. Bernanke and his discretion any day.
Steven Kaplan
Key Largo, Fla.
---
Mr. Taylor appropriately questions the Fed's dual mandate of full employment and stable prices. While
inflation affects 100% of the public, unemployment currently only affects 8.3% of the work force.
Perhaps this dual mandate is a relic from the beliefs in the Phillips curve, which has since been disproved.
Mr. Bernanke recently lectured on Okun's Law and the 2-1 effect on GDP and unemployment. Perhaps that
economic principle is also outdated.
The Fed would be well served to reacquaint itself with the well-known Taylor rule developed by the op-ed's
author, rather than rely on discretionary quantitative easing that will impair price stability.

--- Page 96 ---
Page 96 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Frank Caliri III
Whittier, Calif.

Page 97 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
U.S. News: Fed Holds Fire on Stimulus
U.S. News: Fed Holds Fire on Stimulus
By Jon Hilsenrath and Matt Phillips
1,074 words
4 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Fed is in no hurry to launch new measures to boost economic growth, minutes from the central bank's
most recent meeting showed, disappointing investors eager for more stimulus.
Among the hints dropped in minutes of the Fed's March 13 policy gathering, Federal Reserve staff concluded
that the U.S. economy is a little more susceptible to inflation than previously thought. That and other signals
suggested that another round of bond buying by the Fed to push down long-term interest rates isn't
imminent.
The news disappointed financial markets. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell by as much as 1% before
recovering slightly to finish down 64.94 points at 13199.55. Treasurys also fell, sending yields higher.
The minutes, released Tuesday afternoon, with the customary three-week lag following the meeting, noted
that "a couple" of officials said additional stimulus could become necessary if the economy lost momentum or
inflation receded.
But that sounded a bit more timid than a January meeting, when the Fed said "a few" officials thought bond
buying could be needed "before long."
Many investors had hoped that the central bank would soon launch a new round of bond buying to help prop
up markets and drive the economy. While the Fed's minutes on Tuesday didn't dispel the possibility, it
dimmed the prospects of any imminent move, traders said.
"Today really threw a lot of cold water on that view," said Dan Orlando, managing director and head of
government trading at Deutsche Bank AG in New York.
The Fed has pumped trillions of dollars into financial markets since the financial crisis, helping power a rally
in stocks and Treasurys. The Dow has more than doubled from its March 2009 nadir and yields on Treasurys
remain at historically low levels.
Treasury bonds were hit especially hard. As prices fell, the yield on the benchmark 10-year note jumped
roughly 0.09 percentage point, to 2.284%.
The prospect of less Fed involvement than expected buoyed the dollar, which rose 0.65% against the euro
and 0.90% against the yen.
Gold, which had been bought as a protection against inflation and a flood of dollars into the system, dropped
almost 2% in electronic trading.
The Fed remains very cautious about the economic outlook. At the March meeting, officials left short-term
interest rates near zero and said rates would likely stay there until late 2014.
Since last month's gathering, Fed officials have suggested their broader plan is to wait and see how the
economy performs before making any other changes to monetary policy.
In recent weeks, economic benchmarks have signaled a continuing recovery, with manufacturing notching
gains in March as consumer demand picked up. While the ailing housing market has yet to signal a definitive
turn toward health, job gains and spending figures point to an emboldened shopper, unfazed by gas prices
threatening to hit $4 a gallon. Indeed, rising fuel costs are one of the factors behind soaring sales for U.S. car
makers. In March, drivers seeking small and fuel-efficient cars sent new-vehicle sales up 12.7% from a year
ago.
As the recovery has gathered steam through 2012, investors have paid particular attention to comments by
Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke as well as other central-bank policy makers.

--- Page 98 ---
Page 98 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In recent weeks, some investors and analysts had raised the prospect that the Fed could refrain from another
round of quantitative easing, dubbed QE3. Some had also suggested that the economy could strengthen so
much that the Fed would be forced to raise interest rates earlier than planned.
Mr. Bernanke has been careful not to feed those rumors. Just last week, he said the Fed's easy-money
policies were still needed to help resolve problems in the labor market.
One critical unknown that will weigh on the Fed's decision about new actions to spur growth is economic
slack.
When unemployment is high, homes are unoccupied and factories run below capacity, the economy is
believed to have a lot of slack, meaning growth can pick up without straining resources and causing inflation.
Officials who want the Fed to do more believe there is a lot of slack in the economy.
On the other hand, if there is not much slack -- meaning factories are running full tilt and there are few spare
workers available for new jobs -- then inflation pressures could be closer than thought and the Fed has less
leeway to jump-start growth.
Slack has been a puzzle in this recovery. Though there appears to be a great deal of slack in the form of high
unemployment and empty homes, inflation hasn't retreated much.
The central bank's staff plays an important role in shaping its internal debates on this and other issues. Staff
economists make lengthy presentations on the economy and financial markets at every central bank
meeting and help to set the tone of the discussions with their economic projections.
The staff concluded there was still a "wide margin" of slack in the economy but that it was "somewhat
narrower" than previously thought, the minutes showed. One reason there was less slack, the staff said, was
that the overall economy had a little less capacity to produce goods and services than previously thought.
The staff also raised its projection for inflation "a bit," in part because of "a somewhat narrowed margin of
economic slack in the March forecast," the minutes said.
The arguments -- in a small sense -- weigh against the Fed taking new actions to boost growth, such as
launching another bond-buying program.
The minutes are written without specific staff forecasts or naming any officials. Still, they were written in a way
that made clear Fed staff hadn't made dramatic revisions to their forecast.
The minutes showed that Fed officials themselves are very much divided over slack. One Fed official argued
at the meeting that the economy was "much closer" to using up its idle resources than previously thought.
"A few" officials thought the economy had emerged from recession with less potential to grow without causing
inflation.
Other officials said there was still "substantial slack," particularly in the job market. The U.S. unemployment
rate is 8.3%, which is 2.5 percentage points above its average since the 1940s.
---
Mike Ramsey and Neal E. Boudette contributed to this article.

Mexican Peso Garners Votes --- Currency Is Up 9.3% Against the Dollar, Even in Usually Tumultuous Election Year
Currency Trading
Mexican Peso Garners Votes --- Currency Is Up 9.3% Against the Dollar, Even in Usually Tumultuous
Election Year
By Juan Montes
540 words
4 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
MEXICO CITY -- Elections in Mexico usually are a reason to sell the peso.
But this year the currency is at a six-month high against the dollar as campaigning ramps up ahead of a July
1 presidential vote.
Investors say Mexico is likely to avoid the turmoil of past elections, which were accompanied by political
instability that rocked the country's financial system. The front-runners have espoused market-friendly views,
calling for more private investment in the state-controlled energy sector and advocating for a continuation of
the fiscal restraint that has set Mexico apart from many other Latin American nations.
The previous two presidential elections saw the peso lose ground against the dollar ahead of the vote, only to
recover once the elections were over. The peso fell 6% ahead of the 2006 election as investors shunned the
currency on concerns that a left-wing candidate would take office.
This year, though, the peso is up 9.3% against the dollar, making it one of the top-performing
emerging-market currencies after being whipsawed by the European sovereign-debt crisis last year. Late
Tuesday in New York, the Mexican peso fell to 12.76 against the dollar compared with 12.73 on Monday.
The left-wing candidate in 2006, Andres Manuel Lopez Obrador, didn't win but is running again. But investors
aren't spooked; recent polls show the opposition Institutional Revolutionary Party candidate, Enrique Pena
Nieto, in first place. Investors, instead, are focusing on Mexico's light debt load and small budget deficit,
exposure to the U.S. economic recovery and a largely predictable central bank.
"Mexico is not the same country that we saw 10 or 20 years ago," said Francisco Javier Murcio, deputy
portfolio manager for emerging-market strategies at Standish Mellon Asset Management in Boston. "We're
not changing our view that the Mexico peso should keep strengthening this year."
A volatile peso during an election year isn't unprecedented. Devaluations followed elections in 1976 and
1982. And in 1994, Mexico ran out of foreign reserves and was forced to abandon its dollar peg and seek an
international bailout package.
"This time, international investors are less willing to sell assets ahead of elections," said Bryan Carter,
portfolio manager with Acadian Asset Management in Boston, which holds about half a billion dollars of peso
securities. "They have realized that economic foundations aren't really at risk."
Acadian has been overweight peso-denominated assets for several months. "Mexico is the only emerging
market whose economy is accelerating without inflation pressures, which is the ideal scenario for the peso,"
Mr. Carter said.
While it is possible that domestic problems still could impede the peso's rally, some investors say the main
risk comes from outside Mexico. Because the peso is one of the most actively traded emerging-market
currencies, it bears the brunt of any selloff in the sector. Some observers caution that another flare-up in
Europe's sovereign-debt struggles could knock it down again, as risk-averse investors could shun the peso
for assets perceived as safer such as the dollar or gold.

World News: Mali Coup Triggers Curbs
World News: Mali Coup Triggers Curbs
By Drew Hinshaw
428 words
3 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A11
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
DAKAR, Senegal -- West African nations on Monday said they will lock down their borders with Mali and
freeze its account at the region's central bank, in a bid to force out military officers who seized control of one
of the continent's top gold producers.
Ivory Coast President Alassane Ouattara, head of the 15-country Economic Community of West African
States, said the bloc will recall ambassadors from Mali, where junior officers toppled a democratically elected
government in a March 22 coup. West African airports will refuse landing rights to the roughly 20 officers
behind the coup. Starting Tuesday, West African nations will shut their borders with Mali, except for
humanitarian purposes.
Such moves deny the landlocked country access to the seaports it depends on for virtually all of its fuel,
threatening to bring its economy -- and the gold production that helps keep it afloat -- to a halt.
More than 200,000 people have fled drought-choked Mali villages amid food shortages this year, according to
the United Nations. Randgold Resources Ltd. has said its gold mines in Mali had enough diesel to fully
operate for only two weeks.
The sanctions also block Mali's access to the Dakar-based Central Bank for West African States. The bank
manages the West African CFA franc -- pegged to the euro -- used in eight nations, including Mali.
The stiff sanctions are the latest sign that Africa's regional bodies are taking a tougher stance against military
coups and defiant leaders that have often clung to power over this region's still-budding democracies.
When former Ivory Coast President Laurent Gbagbo last year contested an election that the U.N. declared he
had lost, West African neighbors froze the country's account at the central bank, starving the regime of the
revenue it needed to pay its soldiers. Within months, cash supplies dried up and commercial
transactionsstopped.
On Sunday, Capt. Amadou Sonogo, who led Mali's coup, promised to restore constitutional rule. But he has
evaded questions on whether he will remain in power, or when he will hold elections. On Thursday, he
allowed pro-coup protesters to swarm a runway, preventing West African leaders from landing to meet Mali's
new leaders.
Mali's economy was expected to grow 5.5% this year on high gold prices. Instead, the country has been
swamped with an influx of militants from last year's Libyan fighting, who have returned to try to carve out a
new nation in Mali's vast desert north.

World News: Hungary President Quits in Scandal
World News: Hungary President Quits in Scandal
By Gordon Fairclough
425 words
3 April 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Hungary's president resigned from his largely ceremonial post Monday after being stripped of his doctorate in
a plagiarism scandal -- a political embarrassment for Prime Minister Viktor Orban, a close ally whose
controversial agenda the president worked to advance.
Pal Schmitt, elected by Parliament to a five-year term as head of state in 2010, said in an address to
lawmakers that he would step down to prevent his "personal issue" from dividing Hungary.
Mr. Schmitt's decision came after mounting public protests. It was a sharp about-face from his stance Friday,
when he declared in a television interview that he had no intention of leaving his job and instead planned to
write a new dissertation to prove his "perseverance."
While in office, Mr. Schmitt signed into law hundreds of acts of Parliament as well as a new constitution, and
supported legislation put forward by Mr. Orban's Fidesz party that revamped the country's judicial system,
media regulators and other basic institutions.
Critics of Mr. Orban at home and abroad say the moves undermine democratic checks and balances. The
European Union is pressing Hungary to alter some laws and says it won't discuss financial backing for
Hungary until it changes legal provisions the EU says threaten central-bank independence.
Mr. Orban and his allies say they are trying to fix a broken political system that has left the country deeply in
debt. Before his election, Mr. Schmitt pledged support for the government's efforts in "rebuilding the country . .
. economically, socially and morally."
Budapest's Semmelweis University on Thursday withdrew Mr. Schmitt's doctoral degree in physical
education, saying his thesis didn't meet scientific and ethical standards. Academic investigators found large
parts of the paper were copied from other works. The scandal made Mr. Schmitt -- a two-time Olympic gold
medalist and former ambassador to Spain and Switzerland -- a lightning rod for protest by the political
opposition, which has long attacked Mr. Orban and his Fidesz party for choosing candidates for important
posts based on loyalty rather than ability.
In a rare show of unity, activists from opposition parties on the far right and the left joined demonstrations
Friday to call for Mr. Schmitt to resign. Other protesters picketed outside the presidential palace over the
weekend.
On Monday, Mr. Schmitt, 69 years old, denied he had plagiarized and said he would appeal the decision to
take away his doctorate, saying "the process was unethical and unlawful."

REVIEW --- Books -- Five Best: A Personal Choice: Lewis L. Gould --- on outstanding books about presidential campaigning and the electoral...
REVIEW --- Books -- Five Best: A Personal Choice: Lewis L. Gould --- on outstanding books about
presidential campaigning and the electoral process
By Lewis L. Gould
922 words
31 March 2012
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
Copyright 2012 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Realigning America
By R. Hal Williams (2010)
1. Only a handful presidential elections have become the stuff of political folklore. Prominent among them is
the 1896 contest between William McKinley and William Jennings Bryan, known as the "Battle of the
Standards" because the candidates clashed bitterly over monetary policy -- Bryan delivered his famous
"Cross of Gold" speech at the Democratic convention. The common view of the election was that wealthy
businessman Mark Hanna engineered McKinley's election. But legends often mask reality -- the campaign
involved much more than "Cross of Gold" and Mark Hanna. In "Realigning America," R. Hal Williams got the
story of the '96 campaign right at last. As Williams shows, the true architect of the GOP success was not
Hanna but the candidate himself. "All of us want good times," McKinley said, "good wages, good prices, good
markets, and then we want good money too." His program of sound money and tariff protection ended a
generation of stalemated national politics and created a durable Republican majority. Williams's lucid retelling
of the story of McKinley's triumph is a bracing reminder of the intellectual pleasures of synthesis. In a brief but
compelling narrative, the author illuminates how and why Gilded Age Americans voted as they did, but he
also shows us how much campaigns have changed in the long sweep of the nation's history.
Before the Storm
By Rick Perlstein (2001)
2. In November 1964, Lyndon Johnson bestrode the American political world while Barry Goldwater appeared
destined for the ranks of other badly beaten presidential candidates, such as John W. Davis (1924) and Alton
B. Parker (1904). Half a century later, Johnson seems an archaic anomaly of a discredited liberalism.
Goldwater's losing campaign invites continued scrutiny as the harbinger of the modern world of conservatism.
There is no better place to start than Rick Perlstein's "Before the Storm," a lavish re-creation of the 1964
campaign with a focus on Goldwater and conservatism. Perlstein's judgments are sharp and his prose telling:
"The Eastern Establishment had no clothes. But to many it was still garbed in the cloak of limitless power."
Fair and judicious to all the subjects of his narrative, Perlstein achieves the difficult feat of evoking human
sympathy for most of the participants in the events of 1964.
An American Melodrama
By Lewis Chester, Godfrey Hodgson and Bruce Page (1969)
3. The 1968 election brought about the political upheaval that Goldwater's candidacy had foreshadowed four
years earlier. The year's assassinations, antiwar protests, racial unrest, political intrigue, and ethnic and class
divisions have attracted many chroniclers. The best book appeared one year after the tumult of 1968 had
died away. Three British journalists recorded the traumatic events and clash of ideologies that culminated in
the election of Richard M. Nixon. The authors had access to the major players, and the book captures the
campaign from the inside while tracking what the year meant to average citizens. The 1960s began with
Theodore H. White's celebration of American politics in "The Making of the President, 1960." The decade
ended with a masterly book that revealed a troubled society struggling to remain whole.
What It Takes
By Richard Ben Cramer (1992)
4. How do ordinary politicians decide that they have the right stuff to reach the White House? Journalist
Richard Ben Cramer set out to answer that question by chronicling the 1988 campaign. His prizewinning
study delved into the biographies of two Republicans, Robert Dole and George H.W. Bush, as well as four

--- Page 103 ---
Page 103 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Democrats -- Joseph Biden, Richard Gephardt, Gary Hart and Michael Dukakis -- to learn from their life
stories the qualities that lead to success or failure as a presidential candidate. A definitive judgment remained
elusive but Cramer's painstaking research and lively writing established, especially in the case of Bush and
Dole, how draining and dehumanizing the selection process can be. Astringent and perceptive, Cramer
captures his actors with a phrase. "Bush always had [Brent] Scowcroft along now, like a Pocket New World
Order Doll." Cramer's narrative takes the reader inside the personalities of these leaders with striking clarity in
a volume unmatched for its dissection of the election process.
Quest for the Presidency, 1992
By Peter Goldman, Thomas M. DeFrank, Mark Miller, Andrew Murr, Tom Mathews (1994)
5. The election of 1992 was as exciting as the 1988 contest had been dull, and the team assembled by
Newsweek provided a thorough analysis of the result in a comprehensive volume rich in pertinent information.
The rivalry among Bill Clinton, George H.W. Bush and Ross Perot now seems remote from contemporary
concerns. In fact, as the authors show, the trajectory of modern American politics dates back to this pivotal
electoral encounter. It is instructive to remember how polarizing Bill Clinton was during this campaign and
how Republican skepticism about his legitimacy shaped the outcome of his presidency. Ross Perot seems
forgotten now, but his political insurgency may well be seen as a forerunner of the tea party. George H.W.
Bush's doomed re-election also anticipates some of the current divisions within the Grand Old Party. The
campaign documents that the authors assembled make this book almost a primary source about the origins
of modern campaigning.
---
Mr. Gould, a history professor emeritus at the University of Texas in Austin, is the author of 'Theodore
Roosevelt' (Oxford, 2012).
